<style data-mw-deduplicate="TemplateStyles:r1033289096">.mw-parser-output .hatnote{font-style:italic}.mw-parser-output div.hatnote{padding-left:1.6em;margin-bottom:0.5em}.mw-parser-output .hatnote i{font-style:normal}.mw-parser-output .hatnote+link+.hatnote{margin-top:-0.5em}</style><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Not to be confused with <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Roman Empire</a>, <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a>, or <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a>.</div>

<li><a href="/wiki/Palermo" title="Palermo">Palermo</a> from 1194 (coronation of <a href="/wiki/Henry_VI,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry VI, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry VI</a> to the hereditary title of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sicily" title="Kingdom of Sicily">Kingdom of Sicily</a> and Palermo became the seat of the Empire until the fall of the <a href="/wiki/Hohenstaufen" title="Hohenstaufen">Hohenstaufen</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-3"><a href="#cite_note-3">[2]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-4"><a href="#cite_note-4">[3]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-5"><a href="#cite_note-5">[4]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Innsbruck" title="Innsbruck">Innsbruck</a> under Maximilian I (1508-1519) (seat of the <i>Hofkammer</i>  and the Court Chancery)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrady2009211_6-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrady2009211-6">[5]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPavlacLott2019249_7-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPavlacLott2019249-7">[6]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Vienna" title="Vienna">Vienna</a>  from around 1550s, under <a href="/wiki/Ferdinand_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Ferdinand I, Holy Roman Emperor">Ferdinand I</a>, to 1583; from 1612 to 1806 <sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-8"><a href="#cite_note-8">[7]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-9"><a href="#cite_note-9">[8]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-10"><a href="#cite_note-10">[9]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-11"><a href="#cite_note-11">[10]</a></sup> (seat of the Aulic Council since 1497, Imperial Residence since 1556)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-12"><a href="#cite_note-12">[11]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Prague" title="Prague">Prague</a> 1583-1612<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-13"><a href="#cite_note-13">[12]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPavlacLott2019278_14-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPavlacLott2019278-14">[13]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Regensburg" title="Regensburg">Regensburg</a> (<a href="/wiki/Imperial_Diet_(Holy_Roman_Empire)" title="Imperial Diet (Holy Roman Empire)">Imperial Diet</a> from 1594, <a href="/wiki/Perpetual_Diet_of_Regensburg" title="Perpetual Diet of Regensburg">perpetual from 1663</a>)<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Regensburg_15-0"><a href="#cite_note-Regensburg-15">[b]</a></sup></li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Wetzlar" title="Wetzlar">Wetzlar</a> (<a href="/wiki/Reichskammergericht" title="Reichskammergericht">Imperial Chamber Court</a> from 1689)</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Frankfurt" title="Frankfurt">Frankfurt</a> (<a href="/wiki/Imperial_election" title="Imperial election">election</a> and <a href="/wiki/Coronation_of_the_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Coronation of the Holy Roman Emperor">coronation</a> of the emperor from 1562, before different places for the election and mostly <a href="/wiki/Rome" title="Rome">Rome</a> for the coronation)</li></ul>

<p>The <b>Holy Roman Empire</b> (<a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">Sacrum Romanum Imperium</i>; <a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <i lang="de">Heiliges Römisches Reich</i>, <small>pronounced </small><span class="IPA" lang="de-Latn-fonipa" title="Representation in the International Phonetic Alphabet (IPA)"><a href="/wiki/Help:IPA/Standard_German" title="Help:IPA/Standard German">[ˌhaɪ̯lɪɡəs ˌʁøːmɪʃəs ˈʁaɪ̯ç]</a></span> <span class="nowrap" style="font-size:85%">(<span class="unicode haudio"><span class="fn"><span style="white-space:nowrap;margin-right:.25em;"><a href="/wiki/File:De-Heiliges_R%C3%B6misches_Reich2.ogg" title="About this sound"><img alt="" data-file-height="20" data-file-width="20" decoding="async" height="11" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/11px-Loudspeaker.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/17px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/8/8a/Loudspeaker.svg/22px-Loudspeaker.svg.png 2x" width="11"/></a></span><a class="internal" href="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/b/bf/De-Heiliges_R%C3%B6misches_Reich2.ogg" title="De-Heiliges Römisches Reich2.ogg">listen</a></span></span>)</span>) was a political entity<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHardy20183_21-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHardy20183-21">[17]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-22"><a href="#cite_note-22">[18]</a></sup> in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western</a>, <a href="/wiki/Central_Europe" title="Central Europe">Central</a> and <a href="/wiki/Southern_Europe" title="Southern Europe">Southern Europe</a> that developed during the <a href="/wiki/Early_Middle_Ages" title="Early Middle Ages">Early Middle Ages</a> and continued until its <a href="/wiki/Dissolution_of_the_Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Dissolution of the Holy Roman Empire">dissolution in 1806</a> during the <a href="/wiki/Napoleonic_Wars" title="Napoleonic Wars">Napoleonic Wars</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB.HRE_23-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB.HRE-23">[19]</a></sup>
</p><p>From the accession of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Otto_I" title="Otto I">Otto I</a> in 962 until the twelfth century, the Empire was the most powerful monarchy in Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-24"><a href="#cite_note-24">[20]</a></sup> Andrew Holt characterizes it as "perhaps the most powerful European state of the Middle Age".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-25"><a href="#cite_note-25">[21]</a></sup> The functioning of government depended on the harmonic cooperation (dubbed consensual rulership or <i>konsensuale Herrschaft</i> by Schneidmüller) between monarch and vassals<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-26"><a href="#cite_note-26">[22]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-27"><a href="#cite_note-27">[23]</a></sup> but this harmony was disturbed during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Salian_Dynasty" title="Salian Dynasty">Salian</a> period.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-28"><a href="#cite_note-28">[24]</a></sup> The empire reached the apex of territorial expansion and power under the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/House_of_Hohenstaufen" title="House of Hohenstaufen">House of Hohenstaufen</a> in the mid-thirteenth century, but overextending led to partial collapse.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-29"><a href="#cite_note-29">[25]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilson199918_30-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson199918-30">[26]</a></sup>
</p><p>On 25 December 800, <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_III" title="Pope Leo III">Pope Leo III</a> crowned the <a href="/wiki/List_of_Frankish_kings" title="List of Frankish kings">Frankish king</a> <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a> as <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Empire" title="Carolingian Empire">emperor</a>, reviving the title in <a href="/wiki/Western_Europe" title="Western Europe">Western Europe</a>, more than three centuries after the <a href="/wiki/Fall_of_the_Western_Roman_Empire" title="Fall of the Western Roman Empire">fall</a> of the earlier ancient <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Western Roman Empire</a> in 476.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-31"><a href="#cite_note-31">[27]</a></sup> In theory and diplomacy, the emperors were considered <i><a href="/wiki/Primus_inter_pares" title="Primus inter pares">primus inter pares</a></i>, regarded as first among equals among other Catholic monarchs across Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBreverton2014104_32-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBreverton2014104-32">[28]</a></sup> The title continued in the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_dynasty" title="Carolingian dynasty">Carolingian family</a> until 888 and from 896 to 899, after which it was contested by the rulers of Italy in a series of civil wars until the death of the last Italian claimant, <a href="/wiki/Berengar_I_of_Italy" title="Berengar I of Italy">Berengar I</a>, in 924. The title was revived again in 962 when <a href="/wiki/Otto_the_Great" title="Otto the Great">Otto I</a>, King of Germany, was crowned emperor by <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_XII" title="Pope John XII">Pope John XII</a>, fashioning himself as the successor of Charlemagne<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECantor1993212–215_33-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECantor1993212–215-33">[29]</a></sup> and beginning a continuous existence of the empire for over eight centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gascoigne_34-0"><a href="#cite_note-Gascoigne-34">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies1996316–317_35-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies1996316–317-35">[31]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Charlemagne1_36-0"><a href="#cite_note-Charlemagne1-36">[e]</a></sup> Some historians refer to the coronation of Charlemagne as the origin of the empire,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBryce18992–3_37-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBryce18992–3-37">[32]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHeer19671–8_38-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHeer19671–8-38">[33]</a></sup> while others prefer the coronation of Otto I as its beginning.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies1996317,_1246_39-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies1996317,_1246-39">[34]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKleinhenz2004810_40-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKleinhenz2004810-40">[35]</a></sup> <a href="/wiki/Henry_the_Fowler" title="Henry the Fowler">Henry the Fowler</a>, the founder of the <a href="/wiki/East_Francia" title="East Francia">medieval German state</a> (ruled 919 – 936),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEPavlacLott2019229_41-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEPavlacLott2019229-41">[36]</a></sup> has sometimes been considered the founder of the Empire as well.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-42"><a href="#cite_note-42">[37]</a></sup> The modern view favours Otto as the true founder.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-43"><a href="#cite_note-43">[38]</a></sup> Scholars generally concur in relating an evolution of the institutions and principles constituting the empire, describing a gradual assumption of the imperial title and role.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhaley2012a17–21_44-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhaley2012a17–21-44">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBryce18992–3_37-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBryce18992–3-37">[32]</a></sup>
</p><p>The exact term "Holy Roman Empire" was not used until the 13th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaripzanov2008_45-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaripzanov2008-45">[40]</a></sup> but the Emperor's legitimacy always rested on the concept of <i><a href="/wiki/Translatio_imperii" title="Translatio imperii">translatio imperii</a></i>, that he held supreme power inherited from the ancient emperors of <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Rome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhaley2012a17–21_44-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhaley2012a17–21-44">[39]</a></sup> The imperial office was traditionally elective through the mostly German <a href="/wiki/Prince-elector" title="Prince-elector">prince-electors</a>.
</p><p>During the final phase of the reign of Emperor <a href="/wiki/Frederick_III,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick III, Holy Roman Emperor">Frederick III</a> (ruled 1452–1493), <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Reform" title="Imperial Reform">Imperial Reform</a> began. The reform would largely be materialized during <a href="/wiki/Maximilian_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Maximilian I, Holy Roman Emperor">Maximilian I</a>'s rule (from 1486 as King of the Romans, from 1493 as sole ruler, and from 1508 as Holy Roman Emperor, until his death in 1519). The Empire transformed into the Holy Roman Empire of the German nation. It was during this time that the Empire gained most of its institutions that endured until its final demise in the nineteenth century.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilson2016b79_46-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson2016b79-46">[41]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrady2009104–106_47-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrady2009104–106-47">[42]</a></sup> Thomas Brady Jr. opines that the Imperial Reform was successful, although perhaps at the expense of the reform of the Church, partly because Maximilian was not really serious about the religious matter.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrady2009128,_129,_144_48-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrady2009128,_129,_144-48">[43]</a></sup>
</p><p>According to Brady Jr., the Empire, after the Imperial Reform, was a political body of remarkable longevity and stability, and "resembled in some respects the monarchical polities of Europe's western tier, and in others the loosely integrated, elective polities of East Central Europe." The new corporate German Nation, instead of simply obeying the emperor, negotiated with him.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBrady2009128,_129_49-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrady2009128,_129-49">[44]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson199623_50-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson199623-50">[45]</a></sup> On 6 August 1806,  Emperor <a href="/wiki/Francis_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Francis II, Holy Roman Emperor">Francis II</a> dissolved the empire following the creation of the <a href="/wiki/Confederation_of_the_Rhine" title="Confederation of the Rhine">Confederation of the Rhine</a> by <a href="/wiki/Emperor_of_the_French" title="Emperor of the French">Emperor of the French</a> <a href="/wiki/Napoleon" title="Napoleon">Napoleon I</a> the month before.
</p>

<h2><span class="mw-headline" id="Name_and_general_perception">Name and general perception</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=1" title="Edit section: Name and general perception">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h2>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Reich" title="Reich">Reich</a>, <a href="/wiki/Translatio_imperii" title="Translatio imperii">Translatio imperii</a>, <a href="/wiki/Problem_of_two_emperors" title="Problem of two emperors">Problem of two emperors</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Historiography_of_Germany" title="Historiography of Germany">Historiography of Germany</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:252px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Quaterionenadler_David_de_Negker.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="2792" data-file-width="3586" decoding="async" height="195" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Quaterionenadler_David_de_Negker.jpg/250px-Quaterionenadler_David_de_Negker.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Quaterionenadler_David_de_Negker.jpg/375px-Quaterionenadler_David_de_Negker.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/e/e5/Quaterionenadler_David_de_Negker.jpg/500px-Quaterionenadler_David_de_Negker.jpg 2x" width="250"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Quaterionenadler_David_de_Negker.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The double-headed eagle with coats of arms of individual states, the symbol of the Holy Roman Empire (painting from 1510)</div></div></div>
<p>The Empire was considered by the Roman Catholic Church to be the only legal successor of the Roman Empire during the Middle Ages and the early modern period.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (April 2022)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> Since Charlemagne, the realm was merely referred to as the <i>Roman Empire</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilson19992_51-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson19992-51">[46]</a></sup> The term <i>sacrum</i> ("holy", in the sense of "consecrated") in connection with the medieval Roman Empire was used beginning in 1157 under <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frederick_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick I, Holy Roman Emperor">Frederick I Barbarossa</a> ("Holy Empire"): the term was added to reflect Frederick's ambition to dominate Italy and the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Papacy" title="Papacy">Papacy</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whaley17_52-0"><a href="#cite_note-Whaley17-52">[47]</a></sup> The form "Holy Roman Empire" is attested from 1254 onward.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMoraw1999col._2025–2028_53-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoraw1999col._2025–2028-53">[48]</a></sup>
</p><p>The exact term "Holy Roman Empire" was not used until the 13th century, before which the empire was referred to variously as <i>universum regnum</i> ("the whole kingdom", as opposed to the regional kingdoms), <i>imperium christianum</i> ("Christian empire"), or <i>Romanum imperium</i> ("Roman empire"),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEGaripzanov2008_45-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGaripzanov2008-45">[40]</a></sup> but the Emperor's legitimacy always rested on the concept of <i><a href="/wiki/Translatio_imperii" title="Translatio imperii">translatio imperii</a></i>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-54"><a href="#cite_note-54">[f]</a></sup> that he held supreme power inherited from the ancient emperors of <a href="/wiki/Roman_Empire" title="Roman Empire">Rome</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhaley2012a17–21_44-2"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhaley2012a17–21-44">[39]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a decree following the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Diet_(Holy_Roman_Empire)#Locations_of_Imperial_Diets" title="Imperial Diet (Holy Roman Empire)">Diet of Cologne</a> in 1512, the name was changed to the <b>Holy Roman Empire of the German Nation</b> (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <i lang="de">Heiliges Römisches Reich Deutscher Nation</i>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Latin_language" title="Latin language">Latin</a>: <i lang="la">Sacrum Imperium Romanum Nationis Germanicæ</i>),<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilson19992_51-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson19992-51">[46]</a></sup> a form first used in a document in 1474.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Whaley17_52-1"><a href="#cite_note-Whaley17-52">[47]</a></sup> The new title was adopted partly because the Empire lost most of its territories in Italy and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Arles" title="Kingdom of Arles">Burgundy</a> to the south and west by the late 15th century,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-55"><a href="#cite_note-55">[49]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed. (September 2021)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> but also to emphasize the new importance of the German <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Estate" title="Imperial Estate">Imperial Estates</a> in ruling the Empire due to the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Reform" title="Imperial Reform">Imperial Reform</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESchulze199852–55_56-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchulze199852–55-56">[50]</a></sup> The Hungarian denomination "German Roman Empire" (<a href="/wiki/Hungarian_language" title="Hungarian language">Hungarian</a>: <i lang="hu">Német-római Birodalom</i>) is the shortening of this.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-57"><a href="#cite_note-57">[51]</a></sup>
</p><p>By the end of the 18th century, the term "Holy Roman Empire of the German Nation" fell out of official use. Contradicting the traditional view concerning that designation, Hermann Weisert has argued in a study on imperial titulature that, despite the claims of many textbooks, the name <i>"Holy Roman Empire of the German Nation"</i> never had an official status and points out that documents were thirty times as likely to omit the national suffix as include it.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWilson2006719_58-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWilson2006719-58">[52]</a></sup>
</p><p>In a famous assessment of the name, the political philosopher <a href="/wiki/Voltaire" title="Voltaire">Voltaire</a> remarked sardonically: "This body which was called and which still calls itself the Holy Roman Empire was in no way holy, nor Roman, nor an empire."<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEVoltaire1773338_59-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEVoltaire1773338-59">[53]</a></sup>
</p><p>In the modern period, the Empire was often informally called the <i>German Empire</i> (<span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Deutsches Reich</i></span>) or <i>Roman-German Empire</i> (<span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">Römisch-Deutsches Reich</i></span>).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJorioBraun2016_60-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJorioBraun2016-60">[54]</a></sup> After its dissolution through the end of the <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a>, it was often called "the old Empire" (<span title="German-language text"><i lang="de">das alte Reich</i></span>). Beginning in 1923, early twentieth-century German nationalists and <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Party" title="Nazi Party">Nazi Party</a> propaganda would identify the Holy Roman Empire as the "First" Reich (<i>Erstes Reich</i>, <i>Reich</i> meaning empire), with the <a href="/wiki/German_Empire" title="German Empire">German Empire</a> as the "Second" Reich and what would eventually become <a href="/wiki/Nazi_Germany" title="Nazi Germany">Nazi Germany</a> as the "Third" Reich.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTELauryssens1999102_61-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTELauryssens1999102-61">[55]</a></sup>
</p><p>David S.Bachrach opines that the Ottonian kings, above all <a href="/wiki/Henry_the_Fowler" title="Henry the Fowler">Henry the Fowler</a> and <a href="/wiki/Otto_the_Great" title="Otto the Great">Otto the Great</a>, actually built their empire (which became the hegemonic state of Western Europe, with the leading role of the Kingdom of Germany) on the back of military and bureaucratic apparati as well as cultural legacy they inherited from the Carolingians, who ultimately inherited these from the Late Roman Empire:
</p>
<blockquote><p>Consequently, Henry I and Otto I did not begin <i>de novo</i> to develop a military, administrative, and intellectual infrastructure for their kingdom and empire. They built upon the existing structures that they had inherited from their Carolingian predecessors. An argument for continuity should not, however, be confused with a claim for stasis. The Ottonians, just like their Carolingian predecessors, developed and refined their material, cultural, intellectual, and administrative inheritance in ways that fit their own time. It was the success of the Ottonians in molding the raw materials bequeathed to them into a formidable military machine that made possible the establishment of Germany as the preeminent kingdom in Europe from the tenth through the mid-thirteenth century [...] the Carolingians built upon the military organization that they had inherited from their Merovingian and ultimately late-Roman predecessors."</p></blockquote>
<p>Bachrach argues that the Ottonian empire was hardly an archaic kingdom of primitive Germans, maintained by personal relationships only and driven by the desire of the magnates to plunder and divide the rewards among themselves (as argued by Timothy Reuter), but instead, notable for their abilities to amass sophisticated economic, administrative, educational and cultural resources that they utilized to serve their enormous war machine.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-62"><a href="#cite_note-62">[56]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-63"><a href="#cite_note-63">[57]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-64"><a href="#cite_note-64">[58]</a></sup>
</p><p>Until the end of the 15th century, the empire was in theory composed of three major blocks – <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy_(Holy_Roman_Empire)" title="Kingdom of Italy (Holy Roman Empire)">Italy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Germany" title="Kingdom of Germany">Germany</a> and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Arles" title="Kingdom of Arles">Burgundy</a>. Later territorially only the Kingdom of Germany and Bohemia remained, with the Burgundian territories lost to <a href="/wiki/France_in_the_Middle_Ages" title="France in the Middle Ages">France</a>. Although the Italian territories were formally part of the empire, the territories were ignored in the <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Reform" title="Imperial Reform">Imperial Reform</a> and splintered into numerous <a href="/wiki/De_facto" title="De facto">de facto</a> independent territorial entities.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBryce1899183_65-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBryce1899183-65">[59]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEWhaley2012a17–21_44-3"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWhaley2012a17–21-44">[39]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson199623_50-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson199623-50">[45]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB.Italy_66-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB.Italy-66">[60]</a></sup> The status of Italy in particular varied throughout the 16th to 18th centuries. Some territories like <a href="/wiki/Savoyard_state" title="Savoyard state">Piedmont-Savoy</a> became increasingly independent, while others became more dependent due to the extinction of their ruling noble houses causing these territories to often fall under the dominions of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Habsburg" title="House of Habsburg">Habsburgs</a> and their <a href="/wiki/Cadet_branch" title="Cadet branch">cadet branches</a>. Barring <a href="/wiki/Treaties_of_Nijmegen" title="Treaties of Nijmegen">the loss of Franche-Comté in 1678</a>, the external borders of the Empire did not change noticeably from the <a href="/wiki/Peace_of_Westphalia" title="Peace of Westphalia">Peace of Westphalia</a> – which acknowledged the exclusion of Switzerland and the Northern Netherlands, and the French protectorate over Alsace – to the dissolution of the Empire. At the conclusion of the Napoleonic Wars in 1815, most of the Holy Roman Empire was included in the <a href="/wiki/German_Confederation" title="German Confederation">German Confederation</a>, with the main exceptions being the Italian states.
</p>

<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Early_Middle_Ages">Early Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=3" title="Edit section: Early Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Carolingian_period">Carolingian period</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=4" title="Edit section: Carolingian period">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Empire" title="Carolingian Empire">Carolingian Empire</a></div>
<div class="thumb tleft"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Francia_814.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="380" data-file-width="500" decoding="async" height="167" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Francia_814.svg/220px-Francia_814.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Francia_814.svg/330px-Francia_814.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/66/Francia_814.svg/440px-Francia_814.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Francia_814.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>A map of the Carolingian Empire (a.k.a. Francia, the Frankish Empire) within Europe circa 814 AD.</div></div></div>
<p>As <a href="/wiki/Western_Roman_Empire" title="Western Roman Empire">Roman</a> power in <a href="/wiki/Gaul" title="Gaul">Gaul</a> declined during the 5th century, local Germanic tribes assumed control.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEInnes2000167–170_67-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEInnes2000167–170-67">[61]</a></sup> In the late 5th and early 6th centuries, the <a href="/wiki/Merovingian_dynasty" title="Merovingian dynasty">Merovingians</a>, under <a href="/wiki/Clovis_I" title="Clovis I">Clovis I</a> and his successors, consolidated <a href="/wiki/Franks" title="Franks">Frankish</a> tribes and extended hegemony over others to gain control of northern Gaul and the middle <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rhine_river" title="Rhine river">Rhine river</a> valley region.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-68"><a href="#cite_note-68">[62]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed. (September 2021)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDavies1996232,_234_69-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDavies1996232,_234-69">[63]</a></sup> By the middle of the 8th century, however, the Merovingians were reduced to figureheads, and the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_dynasty" title="Carolingian dynasty">Carolingians</a>, led by <a href="/wiki/Charles_Martel" title="Charles Martel">Charles Martel</a>, became the <i>de facto</i> rulers.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-70"><a href="#cite_note-70">[64]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed. (September 2021)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> In 751, Martel's son <a href="/wiki/Pepin_the_Short" title="Pepin the Short">Pepin</a> became King of the Franks, and later gained the sanction of the Pope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKitterick201848–50_71-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKitterick201848–50-71">[65]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB.Pippin_72-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB.Pippin-72">[66]</a></sup> The Carolingians would maintain a close alliance with the Papacy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-73"><a href="#cite_note-73">[67]</a></sup><sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citing_sources#What_information_to_include" title="Wikipedia:Citing sources"><span title="A complete citation is needed. (September 2021)">full citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p><p>In 768, Pepin's son <a href="/wiki/Charlemagne" title="Charlemagne">Charlemagne</a> became King of the Franks and began an extensive expansion of the realm. He eventually incorporated the territories of present-day France, Germany, northern Italy, the Low Countries and beyond, linking the Frankish kingdom with Papal lands.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEJohnson199622_74-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJohnson199622-74">[68]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEKohn2006113–114_75-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKohn2006113–114-75">[69]</a></sup>
</p><p>Although antagonism about the expense of <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Papacy" title="Byzantine Papacy">Byzantine domination</a> had long persisted within Italy, a political rupture was set in motion in earnest in 726 by the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_iconoclasm" title="Byzantine iconoclasm">iconoclasm</a> of Emperor <a href="/wiki/Leo_III_the_Isaurian" title="Leo III the Isaurian">Leo III the Isaurian</a>, in what <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_II" title="Pope Gregory II">Pope Gregory II</a> saw as the latest in a series of imperial heresies.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEDuffy199762–63_76-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDuffy199762–63-76">[70]</a></sup> In 797, the Eastern Roman Emperor <a href="/wiki/Constantine_VI" title="Constantine VI">Constantine VI</a> was removed from the throne by his mother <a href="/wiki/Irene_of_Athens" title="Irene of Athens">Irene</a> who declared herself Empress. As the Latin Church only regarded a male Roman Emperor as the head of <a href="/wiki/Christendom" title="Christendom">Christendom</a>, Pope Leo III sought a new candidate for the dignity, excluding consultation with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Patriarch_of_Constantinople" title="Patriarch of Constantinople">Patriarch of Constantinople</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto2_77-0"><a href="#cite_note-auto2-77">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKitterick201870_78-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKitterick201870-78">[72]</a></sup>
</p><p>Charlemagne's good service to the Church in his defense of Papal possessions against the <a href="/wiki/Lombards" title="Lombards">Lombards</a> made him the ideal candidate. On Christmas Day of 800, Pope Leo III crowned Charlemagne emperor, restoring the title in the West for the first time in over three centuries.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-auto2_77-1"><a href="#cite_note-auto2-77">[71]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcKitterick201870_78-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcKitterick201870-78">[72]</a></sup> This can be seen as symbolic of the papacy turning away from the declining <a href="/wiki/Byzantine_Empire" title="Byzantine Empire">Byzantine Empire</a> towards the new power of <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Carolingian" title="Carolingian">Carolingian</a> <a href="/wiki/Francia" title="Francia">Francia</a>. Charlemagne adopted the formula <i><a href="/wiki/Renovatio_imperii_Romanorum" title="Renovatio imperii Romanorum">Renovatio imperii Romanorum</a></i> ("renewal of the Roman Empire"). In 802, Irene was overthrown and exiled by <a href="/wiki/Nikephoros_I" title="Nikephoros I">Nikephoros I</a> and henceforth there were two Roman Emperors.
</p><p>After Charlemagne died in 814, the imperial crown passed to his son, <a href="/wiki/Louis_the_Pious" title="Louis the Pious">Louis the Pious</a>. Upon Louis' death in 840, it passed to his son <a href="/wiki/Lothair_I" title="Lothair I">Lothair</a>, who was his co-ruler. By this point the territory of Charlemagne was divided into several territories (<i>cf</i>. <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Verdun" title="Treaty of Verdun">Treaty of Verdun</a>, <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Pr%C3%BCm" title="Treaty of Prüm">Treaty of Prüm</a>, <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Meerssen" title="Treaty of Meerssen">Treaty of Meerssen</a> and <a href="/wiki/Treaty_of_Ribemont" title="Treaty of Ribemont">Treaty of Ribemont</a>), and over the course of the later ninth century the title of Emperor was disputed by the Carolingian rulers of the Western Frankish Kingdom or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Western_Francia" title="Western Francia">West Francia</a> and the Eastern Frankish Kingdom or <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Eastern_Francia" title="Eastern Francia">East Francia</a>, with first the western king (<a href="/wiki/Charles_the_Bald" title="Charles the Bald">Charles the Bald</a>) and then the eastern (<a href="/wiki/Charles_the_Fat" title="Charles the Fat">Charles the Fat</a>), who briefly reunited the Empire, attaining the prize.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins2014131_79-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins2014131-79">[73]</a></sup> In the ninth century, Charlemagne and his successors promoted the intellectual revival, known as the <a href="/wiki/Carolingian_Renaissance" title="Carolingian Renaissance">Carolingian Renaissance</a>. Some, like Mortimer Chambers,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-80"><a href="#cite_note-80">[74]</a></sup> opine that the Carolingian Renaissance made possible the subsequent renaissances (even though by the early tenth century, the revival already diminished).<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-81"><a href="#cite_note-81">[75]</a></sup>
</p><p>After the death of Charles, the Fat in 888 the Carolingian Empire broke apart, and was never restored. According to <a href="/wiki/Regino_of_Pr%C3%BCm" title="Regino of Prüm">Regino of Prüm</a>, the parts of the realm "spewed forth kinglets", and each part elected a kinglet "from its own bowels".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECollins2014131_79-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECollins2014131-79">[73]</a></sup> At this point in time, those crowned emperor by the pope controlled only territories in Italy.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template Template-Fact" style="white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Citation_needed" title="Wikipedia:Citation needed"><span title="This claim needs references to reliable sources. (March 2012)">citation needed</span></a></i>]</sup> The last such emperor was <a href="/wiki/Berengar_I_of_Italy" title="Berengar I of Italy">Berengar I of Italy</a>, who died in 924.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Post-Carolingian_Eastern_Frankish_Kingdom">Post-Carolingian Eastern Frankish Kingdom</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=5" title="Edit section: Post-Carolingian Eastern Frankish Kingdom">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>Around 900, East Francia's autonomous <a href="/wiki/Stem_duchy" title="Stem duchy">stem duchies</a> (<a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Franconia" title="Duchy of Franconia">Franconia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Bavaria" title="Duchy of Bavaria">Bavaria</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Swabia" title="Duchy of Swabia">Swabia</a>, <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Saxony" title="Duchy of Saxony">Saxony</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Lotharingia#Duchy_of_Lotharingia" title="Lotharingia">Lotharingia</a>) reemerged. After the Carolingian king <a href="/wiki/Louis_the_Child" title="Louis the Child">Louis the Child</a> died without issue in 911, East Francia did not turn to the Carolingian ruler of West Francia to take over the realm but instead elected one of the dukes, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Conrad_of_Franconia" title="Conrad of Franconia">Conrad of Franconia</a>, as <i>Rex Francorum Orientalium</i>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylorHansen-Taylor1894117_82-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylorHansen-Taylor1894117-82">[76]</a></sup> On his deathbed, Conrad yielded the crown to his main rival, <a href="/wiki/Henry_the_Fowler" title="Henry the Fowler">Henry the Fowler</a> of Saxony (r. 919–36), who was elected king at the Diet of <a href="/wiki/Fritzlar" title="Fritzlar">Fritzlar</a> in 919.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylorHansen-Taylor1894118_83-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylorHansen-Taylor1894118-83">[77]</a></sup> Henry reached a truce with the raiding <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magyars" title="Magyars">Magyars</a>, and in 933 he won a first victory against them in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Riade" title="Battle of Riade">Battle of Riade</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTETaylorHansen-Taylor1894121_84-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTETaylorHansen-Taylor1894121-84">[78]</a></sup>
</p><p>Henry died in 936, but his descendants, the <a href="/wiki/Ottonian_dynasty" title="Ottonian dynasty">Liudolfing (or Ottonian) dynasty</a>, would continue to rule the Eastern kingdom or the Kingdom of Germany for roughly a century. Upon Henry the Fowler's death, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Otto_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Otto I, Holy Roman Emperor">Otto</a>, his son and designated successor,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHoytChodorow1976197_85-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHoytChodorow1976197-85">[79]</a></sup> was elected King in <a href="/wiki/Aachen" title="Aachen">Aachen</a> in 936.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMagill1998706_86-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMagill1998706-86">[80]</a></sup> He overcame a series of revolts from a younger brother and from several dukes. After that, the king managed to control the appointment of dukes and often also employed bishops in administrative affairs.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECantor1993212–213_87-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECantor1993212–213-87">[81]</a></sup> He replaced leaders of most of the major East Frankish duchies with his own relatives. At the same time, he was careful to prevent members of his own family from making infringements on his royal prerogatives.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-88"><a href="#cite_note-88">[82]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-89"><a href="#cite_note-89">[83]</a></sup>
</p><p><b>Formation of the Holy Roman Empire</b>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Heiliges_R%C3%B6misches_Reich_1000.jpg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="5074" data-file-width="5792" decoding="async" height="175" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Heiliges_R%C3%B6misches_Reich_1000.jpg/200px-Heiliges_R%C3%B6misches_Reich_1000.jpg" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Heiliges_R%C3%B6misches_Reich_1000.jpg/300px-Heiliges_R%C3%B6misches_Reich_1000.jpg 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/9/9c/Heiliges_R%C3%B6misches_Reich_1000.jpg/400px-Heiliges_R%C3%B6misches_Reich_1000.jpg 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Heiliges_R%C3%B6misches_Reich_1000.jpg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Holy Roman Empire during the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Ottonian_Dynasty" title="Ottonian Dynasty">Ottonian Dynasty</a></div></div></div>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:202px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Holy_Roman_Empire_11th_century_map-en.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="1227" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="245" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Holy_Roman_Empire_11th_century_map-en.svg/200px-Holy_Roman_Empire_11th_century_map-en.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Holy_Roman_Empire_11th_century_map-en.svg/300px-Holy_Roman_Empire_11th_century_map-en.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Holy_Roman_Empire_11th_century_map-en.svg/400px-Holy_Roman_Empire_11th_century_map-en.svg.png 2x" width="200"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Holy_Roman_Empire_11th_century_map-en.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Holy Roman Empire between 972 and 1032</div></div></div>
<p>In 951, Otto came to the aid of <a href="/wiki/Adelaide_of_Italy" title="Adelaide of Italy">Adelaide</a>, the widowed queen of Italy, defeating her enemies, marrying her, and taking control over Italy.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECantor1993214–15_90-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECantor1993214–15-90">[84]</a></sup> In 955, Otto won a decisive victory over the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Magyars" title="Magyars">Magyars</a> in the <a href="/wiki/Battle_of_Lechfeld" title="Battle of Lechfeld">Battle of Lechfeld</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMagill1998707_91-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMagill1998707-91">[85]</a></sup> In 962, Otto was crowned emperor by <a href="/wiki/Pope_John_XII" title="Pope John XII">Pope John XII</a>,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMagill1998707_91-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMagill1998707-91">[85]</a></sup> thus intertwining the affairs of the German kingdom with those of Italy and the Papacy. Otto's coronation as Emperor marked the German kings as successors to the Empire of Charlemagne, which through the concept of <i><a href="/wiki/Translatio_imperii" title="Translatio imperii">translatio imperii</a></i>, also made them consider themselves as successors to Ancient Rome. The flowering of arts beginning with Otto the Great's reign is known as the <a href="/wiki/Ottonian_Renaissance" title="Ottonian Renaissance">Ottonian Renaissance</a>, centered in Germany but also happening in Northern Italy and France.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-92"><a href="#cite_note-92">[86]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-93"><a href="#cite_note-93">[87]</a></sup>
</p><p>Otto created the imperial church system, often called "Ottonian church system of the Reich", which tied the great imperial churches and their representatives to imperial service, thus providing "a stable and long-lasting framework for Germany".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Reindel_94-0"><a href="#cite_note-Reindel-94">[88]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-95"><a href="#cite_note-95">[89]</a></sup> During the Ottonian era, imperial women played a prominent role in political and ecclesiastic affairs, often combining their functions as religious leader and advisor, regent or co-ruler, notably <a href="/wiki/Matilda_of_Ringelheim" title="Matilda of Ringelheim">Matilda of Ringelheim</a>, <a href="/wiki/Eadgyth" title="Eadgyth">Eadgyth</a>, <a href="/wiki/Adelaide_of_Italy" title="Adelaide of Italy">Adelaide of Italy</a>, <a href="/wiki/Theophanu" title="Theophanu">Theophanu</a>, <a href="/wiki/Matilda,_Abbess_of_Quedlinburg" title="Matilda, Abbess of Quedlinburg">Matilda of Quedlinburg</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-96"><a href="#cite_note-96">[90]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-97"><a href="#cite_note-97">[91]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-98"><a href="#cite_note-98">[92]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-99"><a href="#cite_note-99">[93]</a></sup>
</p><p>In 963, Otto deposed the current Pope John XII and chose <a href="/wiki/Pope_Leo_VIII" title="Pope Leo VIII">Pope Leo VIII</a> as the new pope (although John XII and Leo VIII both claimed the papacy until 964 when John XII died). This also renewed the conflict with the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Byzantine_Emperor" title="Byzantine Emperor">Eastern Emperor in Constantinople</a>, especially after Otto's son <a href="/wiki/Otto_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Otto II, Holy Roman Emperor">Otto II</a> (r. 967–83) adopted the designation <i>imperator Romanorum</i>. Still, Otto II formed marital ties with the east when he married the Byzantine princess <a href="/wiki/Theophanu" title="Theophanu">Theophanu</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMagill1998708_100-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMagill1998708-100">[94]</a></sup> Their son, <a href="/wiki/Otto_III,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Otto III, Holy Roman Emperor">Otto III</a>, came to the throne only three years old, and was subjected to a power struggle and series of regencies until his age of majority in 994. Up to that time, he remained in Germany, while a deposed duke, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crescentius_II" title="Crescentius II">Crescentius II</a>, ruled over Rome and part of Italy, ostensibly in his stead.
</p><p>In 996 Otto III appointed his cousin <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_V" title="Pope Gregory V">Gregory V</a> the first German Pope.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEMcBrien2000138_101-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcBrien2000138-101">[95]</a></sup> A foreign pope and foreign papal officers were seen with suspicion by Roman nobles, who were led by <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Crescentius_II" title="Crescentius II">Crescentius II</a> to revolt. Otto III's former mentor <a href="/wiki/Antipope_John_XVI" title="Antipope John XVI">Antipope John XVI</a> briefly held Rome, until the <a href="/wiki/Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Holy Roman Emperor">Holy Roman Emperor</a> seized the city.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESladen1914_102-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESladen1914-102">[96]</a></sup>
</p><p>Otto died young in 1002, and was succeeded by his cousin <a href="/wiki/Henry_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry II, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry II</a>, who focused on Germany.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTECantor1993215–17_103-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTECantor1993215–17-103">[97]</a></sup>
Otto III's (and his mentor Pope Sylvester's) diplomatic activities coincided with and facilitated the Christianization and the spread of Latin culture in different parts of Europe.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-104"><a href="#cite_note-104">[98]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-105"><a href="#cite_note-105">[99]</a></sup> They coopted a new group of nations (Slavic) into the framework of Europe, with their empire functioning, as some remark, as a "Byzantine-like presidency over a family of nations, centred on pope and emperor in Rome", has proved a lasting achievement.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-106"><a href="#cite_note-106">[100]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-107"><a href="#cite_note-107">[101]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-108"><a href="#cite_note-108">[102]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-109"><a href="#cite_note-109">[103]</a></sup> Otto's early death though made his reign "the tale of largely unrealized potential".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-110"><a href="#cite_note-110">[104]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-111"><a href="#cite_note-111">[105]</a></sup>
</p><p><a href="/wiki/Henry_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry II, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry II</a> died in 1024 and <a href="/wiki/Conrad_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Conrad II, Holy Roman Emperor">Conrad II</a>, first of the <a href="/wiki/Salian_dynasty" title="Salian dynasty">Salian dynasty</a>, was elected king only after some debate among dukes and nobles. This group eventually developed into the college of <a href="/wiki/Prince-elector" title="Prince-elector">Electors</a>.
</p><p>The Holy Roman Empire eventually came to be composed of four kingdoms. The kingdoms were:
</p>
<ul><li><a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Germany" title="Kingdom of Germany">Kingdom of Germany</a> (part of the empire since 962),</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Italy_(Holy_Roman_Empire)" title="Kingdom of Italy (Holy Roman Empire)">Kingdom of Italy</a> (from 962 until 1801),</li>
<li><a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Bohemia" title="Kingdom of Bohemia">Kingdom of Bohemia</a> (from 1002 as the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Bohemia" title="Duchy of Bohemia">Duchy of Bohemia</a> and raised to a kingdom in 1198),</li>
<li><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Burgundy_(from_933)" title="Kingdom of Burgundy (from 933)">Kingdom of Burgundy</a> (from 1032 to 1378).</li></ul>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="High_Middle_Ages">High Middle Ages</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=6" title="Edit section: High Middle Ages">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Investiture_controversy">Investiture controversy</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=7" title="Edit section: Investiture controversy">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Investiture_Controversy" title="Investiture Controversy">Investiture Controversy</a></div>
<p>Kings often employed bishops in administrative affairs and often determined who would be appointed to ecclesiastical offices.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarraclough1984101–134_112-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarraclough1984101–134-112">[106]</a></sup> In the wake of the <a href="/wiki/Cluniac_Reforms" title="Cluniac Reforms">Cluniac Reforms</a>, this involvement was increasingly seen as inappropriate by the Papacy. The reform-minded <a href="/wiki/Pope_Gregory_VII" title="Pope Gregory VII">Pope Gregory VII</a> was determined to oppose such practices, which led to the <a href="/wiki/Investiture_Controversy" title="Investiture Controversy">Investiture Controversy</a> with <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry IV, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry IV</a> (r. 1056–1106), the King of the Romans and Holy Roman Emperor.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarraclough1984101–134_112-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarraclough1984101–134-112">[106]</a></sup>
</p><p>Henry IV repudiated the Pope's interference and persuaded his bishops to excommunicate the Pope, whom he famously addressed by his born name "Hildebrand", rather than his regnal name "Pope Gregory VII".<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarraclough1984109_113-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarraclough1984109-113">[107]</a></sup> The Pope, in turn, excommunicated the king, declared him deposed, and dissolved the oaths of loyalty made to Henry.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-Gascoigne_34-1"><a href="#cite_note-Gascoigne-34">[30]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarraclough1984109_113-1"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarraclough1984109-113">[107]</a></sup> The king found himself with almost no political support and was forced to make the famous <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Walk_to_Canossa" title="Walk to Canossa">Walk to Canossa</a> in 1077,<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarraclough1984122–124_114-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarraclough1984122–124-114">[108]</a></sup> by which he achieved a lifting of the excommunication at the price of humiliation. Meanwhile, the German princes had elected another king, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Rudolf_of_Swabia" title="Rudolf of Swabia">Rudolf of Swabia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarraclough1984123_115-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarraclough1984123-115">[109]</a></sup>
</p><p>Henry managed to defeat Rudolf, but was subsequently confronted with more uprisings, renewed excommunication, and even the rebellion of his sons. After his death, his second son, <a href="/wiki/Henry_V,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry V, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry V</a>, reached an agreement with the Pope and the bishops in the 1122 <a href="/wiki/Concordat_of_Worms" title="Concordat of Worms">Concordat of Worms</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEBarraclough1984123–134_116-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBarraclough1984123–134-116">[110]</a></sup> The political power of the Empire was maintained, but the conflict had demonstrated the limits of the ruler's power, especially in regard to the Church, and it robbed the king of the sacral status he had previously enjoyed. The Pope and the German princes had surfaced as major players in the political system of the empire.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Ostsiedlung">Ostsiedlung</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=8" title="Edit section: Ostsiedlung">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<p>As the result of <a href="/wiki/Ostsiedlung" title="Ostsiedlung">Ostsiedlung</a>, less-populated regions of Central Europe (i.e. sparsely populated border areas in present-day Poland and the Czech Republic) received a significant number of German speakers. <a href="/wiki/Silesia" title="Silesia">Silesia</a> became part of the Holy Roman Empire as the result of the local <a href="/wiki/Piast_dynasty" title="Piast dynasty">Piast dukes'</a> push for autonomy from the Polish Crown.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-EB.Silesia_117-0"><a href="#cite_note-EB.Silesia-117">[111]</a></sup> From the late 12th century, the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Pomerania" title="Duchy of Pomerania">Duchy of Pomerania</a> was under the <a href="/wiki/Suzerainty" title="Suzerainty">suzerainty</a> of the Holy Roman Empire<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHerrmann1970530_118-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHerrmann1970530-118">[112]</a></sup> and the conquests of the <a href="/wiki/Teutonic_Order" title="Teutonic Order">Teutonic Order</a> made that region German-speaking.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHaffner20196–10_119-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaffner20196–10-119">[113]</a></sup>
</p>
<h3><span class="mw-headline" id="Hohenstaufen_dynasty">Hohenstaufen dynasty</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=9" title="Edit section: Hohenstaufen dynasty">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h3>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:302px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Mitteleuropa_zur_Zeit_der_Staufer.svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="10793" data-file-width="8291" decoding="async" height="391" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Mitteleuropa_zur_Zeit_der_Staufer.svg/300px-Mitteleuropa_zur_Zeit_der_Staufer.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Mitteleuropa_zur_Zeit_der_Staufer.svg/450px-Mitteleuropa_zur_Zeit_der_Staufer.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/21/Mitteleuropa_zur_Zeit_der_Staufer.svg/600px-Mitteleuropa_zur_Zeit_der_Staufer.svg.png 2x" width="300"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Mitteleuropa_zur_Zeit_der_Staufer.svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The Hohenstaufen-ruled Holy Roman Empire and <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Sicily" title="Kingdom of Sicily">Kingdom of Sicily</a>. Imperial and directly held Hohenstaufen lands in the Empire are shown in bright yellow.</div></div></div>
<p>When the Salian dynasty ended with Henry V's death in 1125, the princes chose not to elect the next of kin, but rather <a href="/wiki/Lothair_III,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Lothair III, Holy Roman Emperor">Lothair</a>, the moderately powerful but already old Duke of Saxony. When he died in 1137, the princes again aimed to check royal power; accordingly they did not elect Lothair's favoured heir, his son-in-law <a href="/wiki/Henry_X,_Duke_of_Bavaria" title="Henry X, Duke of Bavaria">Henry the Proud</a> of the <a href="/wiki/House_of_Welf" title="House of Welf">Welf</a> family, but <a href="/wiki/Conrad_III_of_Germany" title="Conrad III of Germany">Conrad III</a> of the <a href="/wiki/Hohenstaufen" title="Hohenstaufen">Hohenstaufen</a> family, the grandson of Emperor Henry IV and thus a nephew of Emperor Henry V. This led to over a century of strife between the two houses. Conrad ousted the Welfs from their possessions, but after his death in 1152, his nephew <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frederick_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick I, Holy Roman Emperor">Frederick I "Barbarossa"</a> succeeded him and made peace with the Welfs, restoring his cousin <a href="/wiki/Henry_the_Lion" title="Henry the Lion">Henry the Lion</a> to his – albeit diminished – possessions.
</p><p>The Hohenstaufen rulers increasingly lent land to <i>ministerialia</i>, formerly non-free servicemen, who Frederick hoped would be more reliable than dukes. Initially used mainly for war services, this new class of people would form the basis for the later <a href="/wiki/Imperial_Knight" title="Imperial Knight">knights</a>, another basis of imperial power. A further important constitutional move at Roncaglia was the establishment of a new peace mechanism for the entire empire, the <a href="/wiki/Landfrieden" title="Landfrieden">Landfrieden</a>, with the first imperial one being issued in 1103 under <a href="/wiki/Henry_IV,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry IV, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry IV</a> at <a href="/wiki/Mainz" title="Mainz">Mainz</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTESmailGibson2009_120-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTESmailGibson2009-120">[114]</a></sup><sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEArnold1995398_121-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEArnold1995398-121">[115]</a></sup>
</p><p>This was an attempt to abolish private feuds, between the many dukes and other people, and to tie the emperor's subordinates to a legal system of jurisdiction and public prosecution of criminal acts – a predecessor of the modern concept of "<a href="/wiki/Rule_of_law" title="Rule of law">rule of law</a>". Another new concept of the time was the systematic founding of new cities by the Emperor and by the local dukes. These were partly a result of the explosion in population; they also concentrated economic power at strategic locations. Before this, cities had only existed in the form of old Roman foundations or older <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Bishopric" title="Bishopric">bishoprics</a>. Cities that were founded in the 12th century include <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Freiburg" title="Freiburg">Freiburg</a>, possibly the economic model for many later cities, and <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a>.
</p><p><a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Frederick_I,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick I, Holy Roman Emperor">Frederick I</a>, also called Frederick Barbarossa, was crowned emperor in 1155. He emphasized the "Romanness" of the empire, partly in an attempt to justify the power of the emperor independent of the (now strengthened) pope. An imperial assembly at the fields of Roncaglia in 1158 reclaimed imperial rights in reference to <a href="/wiki/Justinian_I" title="Justinian I">Justinian I</a>'s <a href="/wiki/Corpus_Juris_Civilis" title="Corpus Juris Civilis">Corpus Juris Civilis</a>. Imperial rights had been referred to as <i>regalia</i> since the Investiture Controversy but were enumerated for the first time at Roncaglia. This comprehensive list included public roads, tariffs, <a href="/wiki/Coining_(mint)" title="Coining (mint)">coining</a>, collecting punitive fees, and the seating and unseating of office-holders. These rights were now explicitly rooted in <a href="/wiki/Roman_law" title="Roman law">Roman law</a>, a far-reaching constitutional act.
</p><p>Frederick's policies were primarily directed at Italy, where he clashed with the increasingly wealthy and free-minded cities of the north, especially <a href="/wiki/Milan" title="Milan">Milan</a>. He also embroiled himself in another conflict with the Papacy by supporting a candidate elected by a minority against <a href="/wiki/Pope_Alexander_III" title="Pope Alexander III">Pope Alexander III</a> (1159–81). Frederick supported a succession of <a href="/wiki/Antipope" title="Antipope">antipopes</a> before finally making peace with Alexander in 1177. In Germany, the Emperor had repeatedly protected Henry the Lion against complaints by rival princes or cities (especially in the cases of <a href="/wiki/Munich" title="Munich">Munich</a> and <a href="/wiki/L%C3%BCbeck" title="Lübeck">Lübeck</a>). Henry gave only lackluster support to Frederick's policies, and, in a critical situation during the Italian wars, Henry refused the Emperor's plea for military support. After returning to Germany, an embittered Frederick opened proceedings against the Duke, resulting in a public ban and the confiscation of all Henry's territories. In 1190, Frederick participated in the <a href="/wiki/Third_Crusade" title="Third Crusade">Third Crusade</a>, dying in the <a href="/wiki/Armenian_Kingdom_of_Cilicia" title="Armenian Kingdom of Cilicia">Armenian Kingdom of Cilicia</a>.<sup class="reference" id="cite_ref-FOOTNOTEHunyadiLaszlovszky2001129_122-0"><a href="#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHunyadiLaszlovszky2001129-122">[116]</a></sup>
</p><p>During the Hohenstaufen period, German princes facilitated a successful, peaceful <a href="/wiki/Ostsiedlung" title="Ostsiedlung">eastward settlement</a> of lands that were uninhabited or inhabited sparsely by <a href="/wiki/West_Slavs" title="West Slavs">West Slavs</a>. German-speaking farmers, traders, and craftsmen from the western part of the Empire, both Christians and Jews, moved into these areas. The gradual <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Germanization" title="Germanization">Germanization</a> of these lands was a complex phenomenon that should not be interpreted in the biased terms of 19th-century <a href="/wiki/Nationalism" title="Nationalism">nationalism</a>. The eastward settlement expanded the influence of the empire to include <a href="/wiki/Pomerania" title="Pomerania">Pomerania</a> and <a href="/wiki/Silesia" title="Silesia">Silesia</a>, as did the intermarriage of the local, still mostly Slavic, rulers with German spouses. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Teutonic_Knights" title="Teutonic Knights">Teutonic Knights</a> were invited to <a href="/wiki/Prussia_(region)" title="Prussia (region)">Prussia</a> by Duke <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Konrad_of_Masovia" title="Konrad of Masovia">Konrad of Masovia</a> to Christianize the <a href="/wiki/Old_Prussians" title="Old Prussians">Prussians</a> in 1226. The <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Monastic_state_of_the_Teutonic_Knights" title="Monastic state of the Teutonic Knights">monastic state of the Teutonic Order</a> (<a href="/wiki/German_language" title="German language">German</a>: <i lang="de">Deutschordensstaat</i>) and its later German successor state of the <a href="/wiki/Duchy_of_Prussia" title="Duchy of Prussia">Duchy of Prussia</a> was never part of the Holy Roman Empire.
</p><p>Under the son and successor of Frederick Barbarossa, <a href="/wiki/Henry_VI,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry VI, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry VI</a>, the Hohenstaufen dynasty reached its apex. Henry added the Norman kingdom of Sicily to his domains, held English king <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Richard_the_Lionheart" title="Richard the Lionheart">Richard the Lionheart</a> captive, and aimed to establish a hereditary monarchy when he died in 1197. As his son, <a href="/wiki/Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick II, Holy Roman Emperor">Frederick II</a>, though already elected king, was still a small child and living in Sicily, German princes chose to elect an adult king, resulting in the dual election of Frederick Barbarossa's youngest son <a href="/wiki/Philip_of_Swabia" title="Philip of Swabia">Philip of Swabia</a> and Henry the Lion's son <a href="/wiki/Otto_IV,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Otto IV, Holy Roman Emperor">Otto of Brunswick</a>, who competed for the crown. After Philip was murdered in a private squabble in 1208, Otto prevailed for a while, until he began to also claim Sicily.<sup class="noprint Inline-Template" style="margin-left:0.1em; white-space:nowrap;">[<i><a href="/wiki/Wikipedia:Please_clarify" title="Wikipedia:Please clarify"><span title="The text near this tag may need clarification or removal of jargon. (May 2021)">clarification needed</span></a></i>]</sup>
</p>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:War_flag_of_the_Holy_Roman_Empire_(1200-1350).svg"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="600" data-file-width="1000" decoding="async" height="132" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/War_flag_of_the_Holy_Roman_Empire_%281200-1350%29.svg/220px-War_flag_of_the_Holy_Roman_Empire_%281200-1350%29.svg.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/War_flag_of_the_Holy_Roman_Empire_%281200-1350%29.svg/330px-War_flag_of_the_Holy_Roman_Empire_%281200-1350%29.svg.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/b/b1/War_flag_of_the_Holy_Roman_Empire_%281200-1350%29.svg/440px-War_flag_of_the_Holy_Roman_Empire_%281200-1350%29.svg.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:War_flag_of_the_Holy_Roman_Empire_(1200-1350).svg" title="Enlarge"></a></div>The <i><a href="/wiki/Flags_of_the_Holy_Roman_Empire#War_flag" title="Flags of the Holy Roman Empire">Reichssturmfahne</a></i>, a military <a href="/wiki/Banner" title="Banner">banner</a> during the 13th and early 14th centuries</div></div></div>
<p><a href="/wiki/Pope_Innocent_III" title="Pope Innocent III">Pope Innocent III</a>, who feared the threat posed by a union of the empire and Sicily, was now supported by Frederick II, who marched to Germany and defeated Otto. After his victory, Frederick did not act upon his promise to keep the two realms separate. Though he had made his son Henry king of Sicily before marching on Germany, he still reserved real political power for himself. This continued after Frederick was crowned Emperor in 1220. Fearing Frederick's concentration of power, the Pope finally excommunicated him. Another point of contention was the Crusade, which Frederick had promised but repeatedly postponed. Now, although excommunicated, Frederick led the <a href="/wiki/Sixth_Crusade" title="Sixth Crusade">Sixth Crusade</a> in 1228, which ended in negotiations and a temporary restoration of the <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Jerusalem" title="Kingdom of Jerusalem">Kingdom of Jerusalem</a>.
</p><p>Despite his imperial claims, Frederick's rule was a major turning point towards the disintegration of central rule in the Empire. While concentrated on establishing a modern, centralized state in Sicily, he was mostly absent from Germany and issued far-reaching privileges to Germany's secular and ecclesiastical princes: in the 1220 <i><a href="/wiki/Confoederatio_cum_principibus_ecclesiasticis" title="Confoederatio cum principibus ecclesiasticis">Confoederatio cum principibus ecclesiasticis</a>,</i> Frederick gave up a number of <i>regalia</i> in favour of the bishops, among them tariffs, <a href="/wiki/Coining_(mint)" title="Coining (mint)">coining</a>, and fortification. The 1232 <i><a href="/wiki/Statutum_in_favorem_principum" title="Statutum in favorem principum">Statutum in favorem principum</a></i> mostly extended these privileges to secular territories. Although many of these privileges had existed earlier, they were now granted globally, and once and for all, to allow the German princes to maintain order north of the Alps while Frederick concentrated on Italy. The 1232 document marked the first time that the German dukes were called <i>domini terræ,</i> owners of their lands, a remarkable change in terminology as well.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Kingdom_of_Bohemia">Kingdom of Bohemia</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=10" title="Edit section: Kingdom of Bohemia">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<div class="thumb tright"><div class="thumbinner" style="width:222px;"><a class="image" href="/wiki/File:Karte_B%C3%B6hmische_Krone.png"><img alt="" class="thumbimage" data-file-height="821" data-file-width="823" decoding="async" height="219" src="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Karte_B%C3%B6hmische_Krone.png/220px-Karte_B%C3%B6hmische_Krone.png" srcset="//upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Karte_B%C3%B6hmische_Krone.png/330px-Karte_B%C3%B6hmische_Krone.png 1.5x, //upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/ff/Karte_B%C3%B6hmische_Krone.png/440px-Karte_B%C3%B6hmische_Krone.png 2x" width="220"/></a> <div class="thumbcaption"><div class="magnify"><a class="internal" href="/wiki/File:Karte_B%C3%B6hmische_Krone.png" title="Enlarge"></a></div><a href="/wiki/Lands_of_the_Bohemian_Crown" title="Lands of the Bohemian Crown">Lands of the Bohemian Crown</a> since the reign of Holy Roman Emperor <a href="/wiki/Charles_IV,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles IV, Holy Roman Emperor">Charles IV</a></div></div></div>
<p>The <a href="/wiki/Kingdom_of_Bohemia" title="Kingdom of Bohemia">Kingdom of Bohemia</a> was a significant regional power during the <a href="/wiki/Middle_Ages" title="Middle Ages">Middle Ages</a>. In 1212, King <a href="/wiki/Ottokar_I_of_Bohemia" title="Ottokar I of Bohemia">Ottokar I</a> (bearing the title "king" since 1198) extracted a <a href="/wiki/Golden_Bull_of_Sicily" title="Golden Bull of Sicily">Golden Bull of Sicily</a> (a formal edict) from the emperor <a href="/wiki/Frederick_II,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Frederick II, Holy Roman Emperor">Frederick II</a>, confirming the royal title for Ottokar and his descendants, and the Duchy of Bohemia was raised to a kingdom. Bohemian kings would be exempt from all future obligations to the Holy Roman Empire except for participation in the imperial councils. <a href="/wiki/Charles_IV,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Charles IV, Holy Roman Emperor">Charles IV</a> set <a href="/wiki/Prague" title="Prague">Prague</a> to be the seat of the Holy Roman Emperor.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Interregnum">Interregnum</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=11" title="Edit section: Interregnum">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Main article: <a href="/wiki/Interregnum_(Holy_Roman_Empire)" title="Interregnum (Holy Roman Empire)">Interregnum (Holy Roman Empire)</a></div>
<p>After the death of Frederick II in 1250, the German kingdom was divided between his son <a href="/wiki/Conrad_IV_of_Germany" title="Conrad IV of Germany">Conrad IV</a> (died 1254) and the <a href="/wiki/Anti-king#Germany" title="Anti-king">anti-king</a>, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Count_William_II_of_Holland" title="Count William II of Holland">William of Holland</a> (died 1256). Conrad's death was followed by the <a href="/wiki/Interregnum" title="Interregnum">Interregnum</a>, during which no king could achieve universal recognition, allowing the princes to consolidate their holdings and become even more independent as rulers. After 1257, the crown was contested between <a href="/wiki/Richard_of_Cornwall" title="Richard of Cornwall">Richard of Cornwall</a>, who was supported by the <a href="/wiki/Guelphs_and_Ghibellines" title="Guelphs and Ghibellines">Guelph party</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Alfonso_X_of_Castile" title="Alfonso X of Castile">Alfonso X of Castile</a>, who was recognized by the Hohenstaufen party but never set foot on German soil. After Richard's death in 1273, <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_I_of_Germany" title="Rudolf I of Germany">Rudolf I of Germany</a>, a minor pro-Hohenstaufen count, was elected. He was the first of the <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Habsburgs" title="Habsburgs">Habsburgs</a> to hold a royal title, but he was never crowned emperor. After Rudolf's death in 1291, <a href="/wiki/Adolf,_King_of_the_Romans" title="Adolf, King of the Romans">Adolf</a> and <a href="/wiki/Albert_I_of_Germany" title="Albert I of Germany">Albert</a> were two further weak kings who were never crowned emperor.
</p><p>Albert was assassinated in 1308. Almost immediately, King <a href="/wiki/Philip_IV_of_France" title="Philip IV of France">Philip IV of France</a> began aggressively seeking support for his brother, <a href="/wiki/Charles,_Count_of_Valois" title="Charles, Count of Valois">Charles of Valois</a>, to be elected the next <a href="/wiki/King_of_the_Romans" title="King of the Romans">King of the Romans</a>. Philip thought he had the backing of the French Pope,
<a href="/wiki/Pope_Clement_V" title="Pope Clement V">Clement V</a> (established at Avignon in 1309), and that his prospects of bringing the empire into the orbit of the French royal house were good. He lavishly spread French money in the hope of bribing the German electors. Although Charles of Valois had the backing of pro-French <a href="/wiki/Heinrich_II_of_Virneburg" title="Heinrich II of Virneburg">Henry, Archbishop of Cologne</a>, many were not keen to see an expansion of French power, least of all Clement V. The principal rival to Charles appeared to be <a href="/wiki/Rudolf_II,_Count_Palatine_of_the_Rhine" title="Rudolf II, Count Palatine of the Rhine">Rudolf, the Count Palatine</a>.
</p><p>But the electors, the great territorial magnates who had lived without a crowned emperor for decades, were unhappy with both Charles and Rudolf. Instead <a href="/wiki/Henry_VII,_Holy_Roman_Emperor" title="Henry VII, Holy Roman Emperor">Henry, Count of Luxembourg</a>, with the aid of his brother, <a class="mw-redirect" href="/wiki/Baldwin,_Archbishop_of_Trier" title="Baldwin, Archbishop of Trier">Baldwin, Archbishop of Trier</a>, was elected as Henry VII with six votes at Frankfurt on 27 November 1308. Though a vassal of king Philip, Henry was bound by few national ties, and thus suitable as a compromise candidate. Henry VII was crowned king at Aachen on 6 January 1309, and emperor by Pope Clement V on 29 June 1312 in Rome, ending the interregnum.
</p>
<h4><span class="mw-headline" id="Changes_in_political_structure">Changes in political structure</span><span class="mw-editsection"><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">[</span><a href="/w/index.php?title=Holy_Roman_Empire&amp;action=edit&amp;section=12" title="Edit section: Changes in political structure">edit</a><span class="mw-editsection-bracket">]</span></span></h4>
<link href="mw-data:TemplateStyles:r1033289096" rel="mw-deduplicated-inline-style"/><div class="hatnote navigation-not-searchable" role="note">Further information: <a href="/wiki/Medieval_commune" title="Medieval commune">Medieval commune</a>, <a href="/wiki/League_of_cities" title="League of cities">League of cities</a>, <a href="/wiki/Hanseatic_League" title="Hanseatic League">Hanseatic League</a>, <a href="/wiki/Swabian_League_of_Cities" title="Swabian League of Cities">Swabian League of Cities</a>, <a href="/wiki/Imperial_immediacy" title="Imperial immediacy">Imperial immediacy</a>, and <a href="/wiki/Feudalism_in_the_Holy_Roman_Empire" title="Feudalism in the Holy Roman Empire">Feudalism in the Holy Roman Empire</a></div>
